href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Scotland</b> was divided into a series of kingdoms in the early <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, i.e. between the end of Roman authority in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">southern and central Britain</a> from around 400 CE and <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">the rise</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">kingdom of Alba</a> in 900 CE. Of these, the four most important to emerge were the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, the Scots of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a>, the Britons of <a href="/wiki/Alt_Clut" class="mw-redirect" title="Alt Clut">Alt Clut</a>, and the Anglian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>. After the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> in the late 8th century, Scandinavian rulers and colonies were established on the islands and along parts of the coasts. In the 9th century, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a> combined the lands of the Scots and Picts to form a single kingdom which constituted the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">kingdom of Scotland</a>.\n</p><p>Scotland has an extensive coastline and vast areas of difficult terrain and poor agricultural land. In this period, more land became marginal due to climate change, resulting in relatively light human settlement, particularly in the interior and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_highlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish highlands">Highlands</a>. Northern Britain lacked urban centres and settlements were based on farmsteads and around fortified positions such as <a href="/wiki/Brochs" class="mw-redirect" title="Brochs">brochs</a>, with mixed-farming primarily based on self-sufficiency. In this period, changes in settlement and colonisation meant that the Pictish and <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> began to be subsumed by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">English</a>, and, at the end of the period, by <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>. Life expectancy was relatively low, leading to a young population, with a ruling aristocracy, <a href="/wiki/Serfdom#Freemen" title="Serfdom">freemen</a>, and relatively large numbers of slaves. Kingship was multi-layered, with different kings surrounded by their war bands that made up the most important elements of armed forces, and who engaged in both low-level raiding and occasional longer-range, major campaigns.\n</p><p>The expansion of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> from the margins of Scotland was key to the development of this period, as it became the religion of many inhabitants. Initially influenced by the Celtic tradition originating from what is now <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, by the end of the era it had become integrated into the organisational structures of the Catholic Church. This period produced some highly distinctive monumental and ornamental art, culminating in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a> style, common across Britain and Ireland. The most impressive structures included nucleated hill forts and, after the introduction of Christianity, churches and monasteries. The period also saw the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_literature" title="Scottish literature">Scottish literature</a> in British, Old English, Gaelic and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> languages.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Picts"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Picts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#D\xc3\xa1l_Riata"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Alt_Clut"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Alt Clut</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Bernicia"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Bernicia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Vikings_and_the_Kingdom_of_Alba"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Vikings and the Kingdom of Alba</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Geography"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Geography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Physical_geography"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Physical geography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Settlement"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Settlement</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Economy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Demography"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Demography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Society"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Society</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Kingship"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Kingship</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Warfare"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Warfare</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Pre-Christian_religion"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Pre-Christian religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Early_Christianisation"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Early Christianisation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Celtic_Christianity"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Celtic Christianity</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Viking_paganism"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Viking paganism</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Art"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Art</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Pictish_stones"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Pictish stones</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Pictish_metalwork"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Pictish metalwork</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Irish-Scots_art"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Irish-Scots art</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Insular_art"><span class="tocnumber">10.4</span> <span class="toctext">Insular art</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Architecture"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Architecture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png/170px-Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png/255px-Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png/340px-Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png 2x" data-file-width="794" data-file-height="1123" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_Medieval_Scotland_areas.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major political centres in early Medieval Scotland</div></div></div>\n<p>As the first half of the period is largely <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric" class="mw-redirect" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> plays an important part in studies of early Medieval Scotland. There are no significant contemporary internal sources for the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, although evidence has been gleaned from lists of kings, <a href="/wiki/Annal" class="mw-redirect" title="Annal">annals</a> preserved in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and from sources written down much later, which may draw on oral traditions or earlier sources. From the 7th century there is documentary evidence from Latin sources including the lives of saints, such as <a href="/wiki/Adomn%C3%A1n" title="Adomn\xc3\xa1n">Adomn\xc3\xa1n</a>\'s <i>Life of St. Columba</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_History_of_the_English_People" title="Ecclesiastical History of the English People">Ecclesiastical History of the English People</a></i>. Archaeological sources include settlements, art, and surviving everyday objects.<sup id="cite_ref-Lang2006pp14-15_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lang2006pp14-15-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Other aids to understanding in this period include <a href="/wiki/Onomastics" title="Onomastics">onomastics</a> (the study of names) \xe2\x80\x93 divided into <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">toponymy</a> (place-names), showing the movement of languages, and the sequence in which different languages were spoken in an area, and <a href="/wiki/Anthroponymy" title="Anthroponymy">anthroponymy</a> (personal names), which can offer clues to relationships and origins.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>By the time of Bede and Adomn\xc3\xa1n, in the late seventh century and early eighth century, four major circles of influence had emerged in northern Britain. In the east were the Picts, whose kingdoms eventually stretched from the river Forth to Shetland. In the west were the Gaelic (<a href="/wiki/Goidelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Goidelic">Goidelic</a>)-speaking people of D\xc3\xa1l Riata with their royal fortress at <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> in Argyll, with close links with the island of Ireland, from which they brought with them the name Scots, originally a term used to describe the inhabitants of Ireland. In the south was the British (<a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Brythonic</a>) Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Alt_Clut" class="mw-redirect" title="Alt Clut">Alt Clut</a>, descendants of the peoples of the Roman-influenced kingdoms of "<a href="/wiki/Y_Gogledd_Hen" class="mw-redirect" title="Y Gogledd Hen">The Old North</a>". Finally, there were the English or "Angles", Germanic invaders who had overrun much of southern Britain and held the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a> (later the northern part of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>), in the south-east, and who brought with them <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Picts">Picts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Picts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg/220px-St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg/330px-St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg/440px-St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St%C3%A8leRocheCelte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The so-called <a href="/wiki/Rosemarkie_sculpture_fragments" title="Rosemarkie sculpture fragments">Daniel Stone</a>, Pictish cross slab fragment found at <a href="/wiki/Rosemarkie" title="Rosemarkie">Rosemarkie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Easter_Ross" title="Easter Ross">Easter Ross</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The confederation of Pictish tribes that developed north of the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> may have stretched up as far as <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It probably developed out of the tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Caledonii" class="mw-redirect" title="Caledonii">Caledonii</a> (whose name continued to be used for at least part of the confederation), perhaps as a response to the pressure exerted by the presence of the Romans to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> They first appear in Roman records at the end of the 3rd century as the <i>picti</i> (the painted people: possibly a reference to their habit of tattooing their bodies) when Roman forces campaigned against them. The first identifiable king of the Picts, who seems to have exerted a superior and wide-ranging authority, was <a href="/wiki/Bridei_I_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridei I of the Picts">Bridei mac Maelchon</a> (<abbr title="reigned circa">r. c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;550\xe2\x80\x9384</span>). His power was based in the kingdom of Fidach, and his base was at the fort of <a href="/wiki/Craig_Phadrig" title="Craig Phadrig">Craig Phadrig</a>, near modern <a href="/wiki/Inverness" title="Inverness">Inverness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> After his death, leadership seems to have shifted to the <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>, whose lands were centred on <a href="/wiki/Strathearn" title="Strathearn">Strathearn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menteith" title="Menteith">Menteith</a> and who raided along the eastern coast into modern England. Christian missionaries from <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> appear to have begun the conversion of the Picts to Christianity from 563.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn the 7th century, the Picts acquired <a href="/wiki/Bridei_III_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridei III of the Picts">Bridei map Beli</a> (671\xe2\x80\x93693) as a king, perhaps imposed by the kingdom of Alt Clut, where his father <a href="/wiki/Beli_I_of_Alt_Clut" title="Beli I of Alt Clut">Beli I</a> and then his brother <a href="/wiki/Eugein_I_of_Alt_Clut" title="Eugein I of Alt Clut">Eugein I</a> ruled.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp.63-5_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp.63-5-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> At this point the Anglo-Saxon kingdom of Bernicia was expanding northwards, and the Picts were probably tributary to them until, in 685, Bridei defeated them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunnichen" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Dunnichen">Battle of Dunnichen</a> in Angus, killing their king, <a href="/wiki/Ecgfrith_of_Northumbria" title="Ecgfrith of Northumbria">Ecgfrith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp.63-5_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp.63-5-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the reign of <a href="/wiki/%C3%93engus_I_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x93engus I of the Picts">\xc3\x93engus mac Fergusa</a> (729\xe2\x80\x93761), the Picts appear to have reached the height of their influence, defeating the forces of D\xc3\xa1l Riata (and probably making them a tributary), invading Alt Clut and Northumbria, and making the first known peace treaties with the English.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Succeeding Pictish kings may have been able to dominate D\xc3\xa1l Riata, with <a href="/wiki/Caustant%C3%ADn_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Caustant\xc3\xadn of the Picts">Caustant\xc3\xadn mac Fergusa</a> (793\xe2\x80\x93820) perhaps placing his son Domnall on the throne from 811.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span id="D.C3.A1l_Riata"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="D\xc3\xa1l_Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: D\xc3\xa1l Riata">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg/220px-Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg/330px-Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg/440px-Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dunadd_Fort_20080427.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> Fort, Kilmartin Glen, probably the centre of the kingdom of D\xc3\xa1l Riata</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a></div>\n<p>The Gaelic overkingdom of D\xc3\xa1l Riata was on the western coast of modern Scotland, with some territory on the northern coasts of Ireland. It probably ruled from the fortress of Dunadd, now near Kilmartin in <a href="/wiki/Argyll_and_Bute" title="Argyll and Bute">Argyll and Bute</a>. In the late 6th and early 7th centuries, it encompassed roughly what is now Argyll and Bute and <a href="/wiki/Lochaber" title="Lochaber">Lochaber</a> in Scotland, and also <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a> in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion1_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> D\xc3\xa1l Riata is commonly viewed as having been an Irish Gaelic colony in Scotland, although some archaeologists have recently argued against this.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The inhabitants of D\xc3\xa1l Riata are often referred to as Scots, from <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">scotti</a></i>, a name used by Latin writers for the inhabitants of Ireland. Its original meaning is uncertain, but it later refers to <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Irish language">Gaelic</a>-speakers, whether from Ireland or elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn 563, a mission from Ireland under <a href="/wiki/St._Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Columba">St. Columba</a> founded the monastery of <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> off the west coast of Scotland, and probably began the conversion of the region to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The kingdom reached its height under <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed%C3%A1n_mac_Gabr%C3%A1in" title="\xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in">\xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in</a> (r. 574\xe2\x80\x93608), but its expansion was checked at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Degsastan" title="Battle of Degsastan">Battle of Degsastan</a> in 603 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfrith_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria">\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria</a>. Serious defeats in Ireland and Scotland in the time of <a href="/wiki/Domnall_Brecc" title="Domnall Brecc">Domnall Brecc</a> (d.&#160;642) ended D\xc3\xa1l Riata\'s golden age, and the kingdom became a client of Northumbria, then a subject to the Picts. There is disagreement over the fate of the kingdom from the late 8th century onwards. Some scholars argue that D\xc3\xa1l Riata underwent a revival under king <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_Find" title="\xc3\x81ed Find">\xc3\x81ed Find</a> (736\xe2\x80\x9378), before the arrival of the Vikings.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alt_Clut">Alt Clut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Alt Clut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alt_Clut" class="mw-redirect" title="Alt Clut">Alt Clut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg/220px-Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg/330px-Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg/440px-Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2707" data-file-height="1605" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Dumbarton_Castle_bordercropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Looking north at <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Dumbarton Rock">Dumbarton Rock</a>, the chief fort of Strathclyde from the 6th century to 870 when it was taken by the Vikings</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Alt Clut</i> (named after the Brythonic name for <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Dumbarton Rock">Dumbarton Rock</a>, the Medieval capital of the <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde" title="Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a> region) may have had its origins with the <a href="/wiki/Damnonii" title="Damnonii">Damnonii</a> people of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geographia_(Ptolemy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographia (Ptolemy)">Geographia</a></i>. Two kings are known from near contemporary sources in this early period. The first is Coroticus or <a href="/wiki/Ceretic_of_Alt_Clut" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceretic of Alt Clut">Ceretic</a> (Ceredig), known as the recipient of a letter from <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a>, and stated by a 7th-century biographer to have been king of the Height of the Clyde, Dumbarton Rock, placing him in the second half of the 5th century. From Patrick\'s letter it is clear that Ceretic was a Christian, and it is likely that the ruling class of the area were also Christians, at least in name.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie1998p8_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie1998p8-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> His descendant <a href="/wiki/Riderch_I_of_Alt_Clut" class="mw-redirect" title="Riderch I of Alt Clut">Rhydderch Hael</a> is named in <a href="/wiki/Adomn%C3%A1n_of_Iona" class="mw-redirect" title="Adomn\xc3\xa1n of Iona">Adomn\xc3\xa1n</a>\'s <i>Life of Saint Columba</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie1998p8_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie1998p8-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nAfter 600, information on the Britons of Alt Clut becomes more common in the sources. In 642, led by <a href="/wiki/Eugein_I_of_Alt_Clut" title="Eugein I of Alt Clut">Eugein</a> son of <a href="/wiki/Beli_I_of_Alt_Clut" title="Beli I of Alt Clut">Beli</a>, they defeated the men of D\xc3\xa1l Riata and killed <a href="/wiki/Domnall_Brecc" title="Domnall Brecc">Domnall Brecc</a>, grandson of \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n, at Strathcarron.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The kingdom suffered a number of attacks from the Picts under \xc3\x93engus, and later the Picts\' Northumbrian allies between 744 and 756. They lost the region of Kyle in the south-west of modern Scotland to Northumbria, and the last attack may have forced the king <a href="/wiki/Dumnagual_III_of_Alt_Clut" title="Dumnagual III of Alt Clut">Dumnagual III</a> to submit to his neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> After this, little is heard of Alt Clut or its kings until Alt Clut was burnt and probably destroyed in 780, although by whom and what in what circumstances is not known,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Historians have traditionally identified Alt Clut with the later Kingdom of Strathclyde, but J. E. Fraser points to the fact there is no contemporary evidence that the heartland of Alt Clut was in <a href="/wiki/Clydesdale" title="Clydesdale">Clydesdale</a> and the Kingdom of Strathclyde may have arisen after Alt Clut\'s decline.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bernicia">Bernicia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Bernicia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Aidan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saint_Aidan.jpg/170px-Saint_Aidan.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saint_Aidan.jpg/255px-Saint_Aidan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saint_Aidan.jpg/340px-Saint_Aidan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="686" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Aidan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Aidan" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Aidan">St. Aidan</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Priory" class="mw-redirect" title="Lindisfarne Priory">Lindisfarne Priory</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Brythonic successor states of what is now the modern Anglo-Scottish border region are referred to by Welsh scholars as part of <i>Yr <a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Hen Ogledd</a></i> ("The Old North"). This included the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bryneich</a>, which may have had its capital at modern <a href="/wiki/Bamburgh" title="Bamburgh">Bamburgh</a> in Northumberland, and <a href="/wiki/Gododdin" title="Gododdin">Gododdin</a>, centred on Din Eidyn (perhaps what is now Edinburgh) and stretching across modern <a href="/wiki/Lothian" title="Lothian">Lothian</a>. Some "<a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>" may have been employed as <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a> along <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian\'s Wall</a> during the late <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a> period. Others are thought to have migrated north (by sea) from <a href="/wiki/Deira_(kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Deira (kingdom)">Deira</a> (<a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a>&#58; <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">Derenrice</i> or <i>Dere</i>) in the early 6th century.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> At some point the Angles took control of Bryneich, which became the Anglo-Saxon kingdom of Bernicia (<a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a>&#58; <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">Beornice</i>). The first Anglo-Saxon king in the historical record is <a href="/wiki/Ida_of_Bernicia" title="Ida of Bernicia">Ida</a>, who is said to have obtained the throne around 547.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Around 600, the Gododdin raised a force of about 300 men to assault the Anglo-Saxon stronghold of <a href="/wiki/Catraeth" class="mw-redirect" title="Catraeth">Catraeth</a>, perhaps <a href="/wiki/Catterick,_North_Yorkshire" title="Catterick, North Yorkshire">Catterick, North Yorkshire</a>. The battle, which ended disastrously for the Britons, was memorialised in the poem <i><a href="/wiki/Y_Gododdin" title="Y Gododdin">Y Gododdin</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIda\'s grandson, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfrith_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria">\xc3\x86thelfrith</a>, united <a href="/wiki/Deira_(kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Deira (kingdom)">Deira</a> with his own kingdom, killing its king <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelric_of_Deira" title="\xc3\x86thelric of Deira">\xc3\x86thelric</a> to form Northumbria around 604. \xc3\x86therlric\'s son returned to rule both kingdoms after \xc3\x86thelfrith had been defeated and killed by the East Anglians in 616, presumably bringing with him the Christianity to which he had converted while in exile. After his defeat and death at the hands of the Welsh and Mercians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hatfield_Chase" title="Battle of Hatfield Chase">Battle of Hatfield Chase</a> on 12 October 633, Northumbria again was divided into two kingdoms under <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> kings. <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a> (r. 634\xe2\x80\x9342), (another son of \xc3\x86thelfrith) defeated the Welsh and appears to have been recognised by both Bernicians and Deirans as king of a united Northumbria. He had converted to Christianity while in exile in D\xc3\xa1l Riata and looked to Iona for missionaries, rather than to Canterbury.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The island monastery of <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a> was founded in 635 by the Irish monk <a href="/wiki/Saint_Aidan" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Aidan">Saint Aidan</a>, who had been sent from Iona at the request of King Oswald. It became the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lindisfarne" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Lindisfarne">Bishop of Lindisfarne</a>, which stretched across Northumbria.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In 638 Edinburgh was attacked by the English and at this point, or soon after, the Gododdin territories in Lothian and around Stirling came under the rule of Bernicia.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989p31_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989p31-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> After Oswald\'s death fighting the Mercians, the two kingdoms were divided again, with Deira possibly having sub-kings under Bernician authority, but from this point the English kings were Christian and after the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a> in 664, the Northumbrian kings accepted the primacy of Canterbury and Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In the late 7th century, the Northumbrians extended their influence north of the Forth, until they were defeated by the Picts at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nechtansmere" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Nechtansmere">Battle of Nechtansmere</a> in 685.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp.63-5_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp.63-5-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vikings_and_the_Kingdom_of_Alba">Vikings and the Kingdom of Alba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vikings and the Kingdom of Alba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">Origins of the Kingdom of Alba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Scotland" title="Scandinavian Scotland">Scandinavian Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norse_activity_in_the_British_Isles" title="Norse activity in the British Isles">Norse activity in the British Isles</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikinger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Wikinger.jpg/170px-Wikinger.jpg" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Wikinger.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="222" data-file-height="337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikinger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Danish seamen, painted mid-12th century</div></div></div>\n<p>The balance between rival kingdoms was transformed in 793 when ferocious Viking raids began on monasteries like Iona and Lindisfarne, creating fear and confusion across the kingdoms of North Britain. Orkney, Shetland and the Western Isles eventually fell to the Norsemen.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The king of Fortriu, <a href="/wiki/Uen_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Uen of the Picts">E\xc3\xb3gan mac \xc3\x93engusa</a>, and the king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_mac_Boanta" title="\xc3\x81ed mac Boanta">\xc3\x81ed mac Boanta</a>, were among the dead after a major defeat to the Vikings in 839.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A mixture of Viking and Gaelic Irish settlement into south-west Scotland produced the <a href="/wiki/Gall-Gaidel" class="mw-redirect" title="Gall-Gaidel">Gall-Gaidel</a>, the <i>Norse Irish</i>, from which the region gets the modern name <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Sometime in the 9th century, the beleaguered kingdom of D\xc3\xa1l Riata lost the Hebrides to the Vikings, when <a href="/wiki/Ketil_Flatnose" class="mw-redirect" title="Ketil Flatnose">Ketil Flatnose</a> is said to have founded the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> These threats may have speeded a long term process of gaelicisation of the Pictish kingdoms, which adopted Gaelic language and customs. There was also a merger of the Gaelic and Pictish crowns, although historians debate whether it was a Pictish takeover of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, or the other way around. This culminated in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenneth I of Scotland">C\xc3\xadnaed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn</a> (Kenneth MacAlpin) in the 840s, which brought to power the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a>, who became the leaders of a combined Gaelic-Pictish kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2006p54_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2006p54-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 867 the Vikings seized Northumbria, forming the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of York">Kingdom of York</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Rollason2003p212_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollason2003p212-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> three years later they stormed the Briton fortress of Dumbarton<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently conquered much of England except for a reduced <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Wessex" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Wessex">Kingdom of Wessex</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rollason2003p212_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollason2003p212-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> leaving the new combined Pictish and Gaelic kingdom almost encircled.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nThe immediate descendants of Cin\xc3\xa1ed were styled either as <i>King of the Picts</i> or <i>King of <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a></i>. They were ousted in 878 when <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_mac_Cin%C3%A1eda" title="\xc3\x81ed mac Cin\xc3\xa1eda">\xc3\x81ed mac Cin\xc3\xa1eda</a> was killed by <a href="/wiki/Giric_mac_D%C3%BAngail" class="mw-redirect" title="Giric mac D\xc3\xbangail">Giric mac D\xc3\xbangail</a>, but returned again on Giric\'s death in 889.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> When C\xc3\xadnaed\'s eventual successor <a href="/wiki/Donald_II_of_Scotland" title="Donald II of Scotland">Domnall mac Causant\xc3\xadn</a> died at <a href="/wiki/Dunnotar_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunnotar Castle">Dunnottar</a> in 900, he was the first man to be recorded as <i>r\xc3\xad Alban</i> (i.e. <i>King of Alba</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Such an apparent innovation in the Gaelic chronicles is occasionally taken to spell the birth of Scotland, but there is nothing extant from or about his reign that might confirm this. Known in Gaelic as "<a href="/wiki/Alba" title="Alba">Alba</a>", in Latin as "<a href="/wiki/Scotia" title="Scotia">Scotia</a>", and in English as "Scotland", his kingdom was the nucleus from which the Scottish kingdom would expand as the Viking influence waned, just as in the south the Kingdom of Wessex expanded to become the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_geography">Physical geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Physical geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pit-placenames1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pit-placenames1.jpg/170px-Pit-placenames1.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pit-placenames1.jpg/255px-Pit-placenames1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pit-placenames1.jpg/340px-Pit-placenames1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="456" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pit-placenames1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the distribution of Pit- place names in Scotland, thought to indicate Pictish settlement</div></div></div>\n<p>Modern Scotland is half the size England and Wales in area, but with its many inlets, islands and inland <a href="/wiki/Lochs" class="mw-redirect" title="Lochs">lochs</a>, it has roughly the same amount of coastline at 4,000 miles. Only a fifth of Scotland is less than 60 metres above sea level. Its east Atlantic position means that it has very heavy rainfall: today about 700&#160;cm (276&#160;in) per year in the east and over 1,000&#160;cm (394&#160;in) in the west. This encouraged the spread of blanket <a href="/wiki/Peat_bog" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bog">peat bog</a>, the acidity of which, combined with high level of wind and salt spray, made most of the islands treeless. The existence of hills, mountains, quicksands and marshes made internal communication and conquest extremely difficult and may have contributed to the fragmented nature of political power.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The early Middle Ages was a period of climate deterioration, with a drop in temperature and an increase in rainfall, resulting in more land becoming unproductive.<sup id="cite_ref-Fouracre&amp;McKitterick2005p.234_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fouracre&amp;McKitterick2005p.234-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Settlement">Settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Roman influence beyond Hadrian\'s Wall does not appear to have had a major impact on settlement patterns, with <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Hill_fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Hill fort">hill forts</a> and promontory forts continuing to be occupied through the early Medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> These often had defences of dry stone or timber laced walls, sometimes with a <a href="/wiki/Palisade" title="Palisade">palisade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Edwards&amp;Ralston2003p224-5_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards&amp;Ralston2003p224-5-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The large numbers of these forts has been taken to suggest peripatetic monarchies and aristocracies, moving around their domains to control and administer them.<sup id="cite_ref-Edwards&amp;Ralston2003p224-5_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards&amp;Ralston2003p224-5-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In the Northern and Western Isles the sites of Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Brochs" class="mw-redirect" title="Brochs">Brochs</a> and wheel houses continued to be occupied, but were gradually replaced with less imposing cellular houses.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> There are a handful of major timber halls in the south, comparable to those excavated in Anglo-Saxon England and dated to the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In the areas of Scandinavian settlement in the islands and along the coast a lack of timber meant that native materials had to be adopted for house building, often combining layers of stone with turf.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Place-name evidence, particularly the use of the prefix "pit", meaning land or a field, suggests that the heaviest areas of Pictish settlement were in modern <a href="/wiki/Fife" title="Fife">Fife</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perthshire" title="Perthshire">Perthshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angus,_Scotland" title="Angus, Scotland">Angus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen" title="Aberdeen">Aberdeen</a> and around the <a href="/wiki/Moray_Firth" title="Moray Firth">Moray Firth</a>, although later Gaelic migration may have erased some Pictish names from the record.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Early Gaelic settlement appears to have been in the regions of the western mainland of Scotland between <a href="/wiki/Cowal" title="Cowal">Cowal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ardnamurchan" title="Ardnamurchan">Ardnamurchan</a>, and the adjacent islands, later extending up the West coast in the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2006p53_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2006p53-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> There is place name and archaeological evidence of Anglian settlement in south-east Scotland reaching into <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian" title="West Lothian">West Lothian</a>, and to a lesser extent into south-western Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Later Norse settlement was probably most extensive in Orkney and Shetland, with lighter settlement in the western islands, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> and on the mainland in Caithness, stretching along fertile river valleys through <a href="/wiki/Sutherland" title="Sutherland">Sutherland</a> and into <a href="/wiki/Ross,_Scotland" title="Ross, Scotland">Ross</a>. There was also extensive Viking settlement in Bernicia, the Northern part of Northumbria, which stretched into the modern <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Borders" title="Scottish Borders">Borders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Lowlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_(languages).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_%28languages%29.png/170px-Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_%28languages%29.png" width="170" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_%28languages%29.png/255px-Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_%28languages%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_%28languages%29.png/340px-Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_%28languages%29.png 2x" data-file-width="597" data-file-height="434" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britons_in_Southern_Scotland_(languages).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Possible language zones in southern Scotland, 7th\xe2\x80\x938th centuries</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">Pictish language</a></div>\n<p>This period saw dramatic changes in the geography of language. Modern linguists divide the Celtic languages into two major groups, the <a href="/wiki/P-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="P-Celtic">P-Celtic</a>, from which <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> derive and the <a href="/wiki/Q-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="Q-Celtic">Q-Celtic</a>, from which comes <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic language">Gaelic</a>. The Pictish language remains enigmatic, since the Picts had no written script of their own and all that survives are place names and some isolated inscriptions in Irish <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">ogham</a> script.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Most modern linguists accept that, although the nature and unity of Pictish language is unclear, it belonged to the former group.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Historical sources, as well as place name evidence, indicate the ways in which the Pictish language in the north and Cumbric languages in the south were overlaid and replaced by Gaelic, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse language">Norse</a> in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg/170px-Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg" width="170" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg/255px-Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg/340px-Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="702" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_Medieval_Economy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of available land in early Medieval Scotland<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Lacking the urban centres created under the Romans in the rest of Britain, the economy of Scotland in the early Middle Ages was overwhelmingly agricultural. Without significant transport links and wider markets, most farms had to produce a self-sufficient diet of meat, dairy products and cereals, supplemented by <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gather" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter-gather">hunter-gathering</a>. Limited archaeological evidence indicates that throughout Northern Britain farming was based around a single homestead or a small cluster of three or four homes, each probably containing a nuclear family, with relationships likely to be common among neighbouring houses and settlements, reflecting the partition of land through inheritance.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007pp17-20_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007pp17-20-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Farming became based around a system that distinguished between the infield around the settlement, where crops were grown every year and the outfield, further away and where crops were grown and then left fallow in different years, in a system that would continue until the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The evidence of bones indicates that cattle were by far the most important domesticated animal, followed by pigs, sheep and goats, while domesticated fowl were rare. Imported goods found in archaeological sites of the period include ceramics and glass, while many sites indicate iron and precious metal working.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Scotland" title="Demography of Scotland">Demography of Scotland</a></div>\n<p>There are almost no written sources from which to re-construct the demography of early Medieval Scotland. Estimates have been made of a population of 10,000 inhabitants in D\xc3\xa1l Riata and 80\xe2\x80\x93100,000 for Pictland.<sup id="cite_ref-Lang2006pp21-2_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lang2006pp21-2-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> It is likely that the 5th and 6th centuries saw higher mortality rates due to the appearance of bubonic plague, which may have reduced net population.<sup id="cite_ref-Fouracre&amp;McKitterick2005p.234_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fouracre&amp;McKitterick2005p.234-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The known conditions have been taken to suggest it was a high fertility, high mortality society, similar to many developing countries in the modern world, with a relatively young demographic profile, and perhaps early childbearing, and large numbers of children for women. This would have meant that there were a relatively small proportion of available workers to the number of mouths to feed. This would have made it difficult to produce a surplus that would allow demographic growth and more complex societies to develop.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007pp17-20_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007pp17-20-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Society_in_Medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Society in Medieval Scotland">Society in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg/170px-Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg" width="170" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg/255px-Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hilton_of_Cadboll_stone_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the Class II <a href="/wiki/Hilton_of_Cadboll_Stone" title="Hilton of Cadboll Stone">Hilton of Cadboll Stone</a>, showing mounted members of the aristocracy</div></div></div>\n<p>The primary unit of social organisation in Germanic and Celtic Europe was the kin group.<sup id="cite_ref-Haigh1999pp82-4_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh1999pp82-4-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The mention of descent through the female line in the ruling families of the Picts in later sources and the recurrence of leaders clearly from outside of Pictish society, has led to the conclusion that their system of descent was <a href="/wiki/Matrilineal" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrilineal">matrilineal</a>. However, this has been challenged by a number of historians who argue that the clear evidence of awareness of descent through the male line suggests that this more likely to indicate a <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_descent" title="Bilateral descent">bilateral</a> system of descent, where descent was counted through both male and female lines.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp57-8_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp57-8-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scattered evidence, including the records in Irish annals and the images of warriors like those depicted on the Pictish stone slabs at <a href="/wiki/Aberlemno" title="Aberlemno">Aberlemno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forfarshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Forfarshire">Forfarshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hilton_of_Cadboll" title="Hilton of Cadboll">Hilton of Cadboll</a> in Easter Ross, suggest that in Northern Britain, as in Anglo-Saxon England, society was dominated by a military aristocracy, whose status was dependent in a large part on their ability and willingness to fight.<sup id="cite_ref-Haigh1999pp82-4_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh1999pp82-4-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Below the level of the aristocracy it is assumed that there were non-noble <a href="/wiki/Serfdom#Freemen" title="Serfdom">freemen</a>, working their own small farms or holding lands as free tenants.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> There are no surviving law codes from Scotland in this period,<sup id="cite_ref-Thronton2009_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thronton2009-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> but codes in Ireland and Wales indicate that freemen had the right to bear arms, represent themselves in law and to receive compensation for murdered kinsmen.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Indications are that society in North Britain contained relatively large numbers of slaves, often taken in war and raids, or bought, as St. Patrick indicated the Picts were doing from the Britons in Southern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Lang2006pp21-2_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lang2006pp21-2-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Slavery probably reached relatively far down in society, with most rural households containing some slaves. Because they were taken relatively young and were usually racially indistinguishable from their masters, many slaves would have been more integrated into their societies of capture than their societies of origin, in terms of both culture and language. Living and working beside their owners they in practice may have become members of a household without the inconvenience of the partible inheritance rights that divided estates. Where there is better evidence from England and elsewhere, it was common for such slaves who survived to middle age to gain their freedom, with such freedmen often remaining clients of the families of their former masters.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007pp17-20_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007pp17-20-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingship">Kingship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Kingship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_in_Medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Government in Medieval Scotland">Government in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg/220px-Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg/330px-Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg/440px-Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1248" data-file-height="1008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Footprint (replica<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>) used in king-making ceremonies at <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the early Medieval period, British kingship was not inherited in a direct line from previous kings, as would be the case in the late Middle Ages. There were instead a number of candidates for kingship, who usually needed to be a member of a particular dynasty and to claim descent from a particular ancestor.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Kingship could be multi-layered and very fluid. The Pictish kings of Fortriu were probably acting as overlords of other Pictish kings for much of this period and occasionally were able to assert an overlordship over non-Pictish kings, but occasionally themselves had to acknowledge the overlordship of external rulers, both Anglian and British. Such relationships may have placed obligations to pay tribute or to supply armed forces. After a victory, sub-kings may have received rewards in return for this service. Interaction with and intermarriage into the ruling families of subject kingdoms may have opened the way to absorption of such sub-kingdoms and, although there might be later overturnings of these mergers, it is likely that a complex process by which kingship was gradually monopolised by a handful of the most powerful dynasties was taking place.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2009_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2009-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The primary role of the king was to act as a war leader, reflected in the very small number of minority or female reigning monarchs in the period. Kings organised the defence of their people\'s lands, property and persons and negotiated with other kings to secure these things. If they failed to do so, the settlements might be raided, destroyed or annexed, and the populations killed or taken into slavery. Kings also engaged in the low level warfare of raiding and the more ambitious full-scale warfare that led to conflicts of large armies and alliances, and which could be undertaken over relatively large distances, such as the expedition to Orkney by D\xc3\xa1l Riata in 581 or the Northumbrian attack on Ireland in 684.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2009_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2009-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Kingship had its ritual aspects. The kings of D\xc3\xa1l Riata were inaugurated by putting their foot in a footprint carved in stone, signifying that they would follow in the footsteps of their predecessors.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p125_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p125-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The kingship of the unified kingdom of Alba had <a href="/wiki/Scone,_Perth_and_Kinross" class="mw-redirect" title="Scone, Perth and Kinross">Scone</a> and its sacred stone at the heart of its coronation ceremony, which historians presume was inherited from Pictish practices. Iona, the early centre of Scottish Christianity, became the burial site of the early kings of Scotland until the eleventh century, when the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Canmore" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Canmore">House of Canmore</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_Abbey" title="Dunfermline Abbey">Dunfermline</a> near Scone.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Warfare_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Warfare in Medieval Scotland">Warfare in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg/170px-Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg" width="170" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg/255px-Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg/340px-Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="1470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pictish_Stone_at_Aberlemno_Church_Yard_-_Battle_Scene_Detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The battle scene from the <a href="/wiki/Aberlemno" title="Aberlemno">Aberlemno</a> <a href="/wiki/Pictish_stone" title="Pictish stone">Pictish stone</a>, generally presumed to show the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunnichen" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Dunnichen">Battle of Dunnichen</a> in 685</div></div></div>\n<p>At the most basic level, a king\'s power rested on the existence of his bodyguard or war-band. In the British language, this was called the <i>teulu</i>, as in <i>teulu Dewr</i> (the "War-band of Deira"). In Latin the word is either <i>comitatus</i> or <i>tutores</i>, or even <i>familia</i>; <i>tutores</i> is the most common word in this period, and derives for the Latin verb <i>tueor</i>, meaning "defend, preserve from danger".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The war-band functioned as an extension of the ruler\'s legal person, and was the core of the larger armies that were mobilised from time to time for campaigns of significant size. In peace-time, the war-band\'s activity was centred on the "Great Hall". Here, in both Germanic and Celtic cultures, the feasting, drinking and other forms of male bonding that kept up the war-band\'s integrity would take place. In the epic poem <a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a>, the war-band was said to sleep in the Great Hall after the lord had retired to his adjacent bedchamber.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It is not likely that any war-band in the period exceeded 120\xe2\x80\x93150 men, as no hall structure having a capacity larger than this has been found by archaeologists in northern Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Pictish stones, like that at <a href="/wiki/Aberlemno" title="Aberlemno">Aberlemno</a> in Angus, show mounted and foot warriors with swords, spears, bows, helmets and shields.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2009_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2009-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The large number of hill forts in Scotland may have made open battle less important than in Anglo-Saxon England and the relatively high proportion of kings who are recorded as dying in fires or drowning suggest that sieges were a more important part of warfare in Northern Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2009_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2009-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sea power may also have been important. Irish annals record an attack by the Picts on Orkney in 682, which must have necessitated a large naval force:<sup id="cite_ref-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> they also lost 150 ships in a disaster in 729.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Ships were also vital in the amphibious warfare in the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands" title="Highlands and Islands">Highlands and Islands</a> and from the seventh century the <i><a href="/wiki/Senchus_fer_n-Alban" title="Senchus fer n-Alban">Senchus fer n-Alban</a></i> indicates that D\xc3\xa1l Riata had a ship-muster system that obliged groups of households to produce a total of 177 ships and 2,478 men. The same source mentions the first recorded naval battle around the British Isles in 719 and eight naval expeditions between 568 and 733.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The only vessels to survive from this period are <a href="/wiki/Dugout_canoe" title="Dugout canoe">dugout canoes</a>, but images from the period suggest that there may have been skin boats (similar to the Irish <a href="/wiki/Currach" title="Currach">currach</a>) and larger oared vessels.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The Viking raids and invasions of the British Isles were based on superior seapower. The key to their success, was a graceful, long, narrow, light, wooden boat with a shallow draft hull designed for speed. This shallow draft allowed navigation in waters only 3 feet (1&#160;m) deep and permitted beach landings, while its light weight enabled it to be carried over <a href="/wiki/Portage" title="Portage">portages</a>. Longships were also double-ended, the symmetrical bow and stern allowing the ship to reverse direction quickly without having to turn around.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Christianity in Medieval Scotland">Christianity in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg/170px-Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg/255px-Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg/340px-Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iona_Abbey_Celtic_Cross.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>St. John\'s cross which stood outside <a href="/wiki/Iona_Abbey" title="Iona Abbey">Iona Abbey</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Christian_religion">Pre-Christian religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pre-Christian religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">Celtic polytheism</a></div>\n<p>Very little is known about religion in Scotland before the arrival of Christianity. The lack of native written sources among the Picts means that it can only be judged from parallels elsewhere, occasional surviving archaeological evidence and hostile accounts of later Christian writers. It is generally presumed to have resembled <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">Celtic polytheism</a>. The names of more than two hundred Celtic deities have been noted, some of which, like <a href="/wiki/Lugh" title="Lugh">Lugh</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Dagda" title="The Dagda">The Dagda</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Morrigan" class="mw-redirect" title="The Morrigan">The Morrigan</a>, come from later Irish mythology, whilst others, like <a href="/wiki/Teutatis" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutatis">Teutatis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taranis" title="Taranis">Taranis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cernunnos" title="Cernunnos">Cernunnos</a>, come from evidence from <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The Celtic pagans constructed temples and shrines to venerate these gods, something they did through <a href="/wiki/Votive_offerings" class="mw-redirect" title="Votive offerings">votive offerings</a> and performing sacrifices, possibly including <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifice</a>. According to Greek and Roman accounts, in Gaul, Britain and Ireland, there was a priestly caste of "magico-religious specialists" known as the <a href="/wiki/Druids" class="mw-redirect" title="Druids">druids</a>, although very little is definitely known about them.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Irish legends about the origin of the Picts and stories from the life of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Ninian" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Ninian">St. Ninian</a>, associate the Picts with druids. The Picts are also associated with "demon" worship and one story concerning St Columba has him exorcising a demon from a well in Pictland, suggesting that the worship of well spirits was a feature of Pictish paganism. Roman mentions of the worship of the Goddess <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> at wells and a Pictish stone associated with a well near <a href="/wiki/Dunvegan_Castle" title="Dunvegan Castle">Dunvegan Castle</a> on Skye have been taken to support this case.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Christianisation">Early Christianisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Early Christianisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Scotland" title="Christianisation of Scotland">Christianisation of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brecbennoch.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Brecbennoch.PNG/220px-Brecbennoch.PNG" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Brecbennoch.PNG/330px-Brecbennoch.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Brecbennoch.PNG/440px-Brecbennoch.PNG 2x" data-file-width="786" data-file-height="482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brecbennoch.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Monymusk_Reliquary" title="Monymusk Reliquary">Monymusk Reliquary</a>, or <i>Brecbennoch</i> as it was called, dates from c. 750, and purportedly enclosed bones of <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a>, the most popular saint in Medieval Scotland</div></div></div>\n<p>The roots of Christianity in Scotland can probably be found among the soldiers and ordinary Roman citizens in the vicinity of Hadrian\'s Wall.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The archaeology of the Roman period indicates that the northern parts
