of the Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a> were among the most Christianised in the island.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chi-Rho" class="mw-redirect" title="Chi-Rho">Chi-Rho</a> inscriptions and Christian grave-slabs have been found on the wall from the 4th century, and from the same period the <a href="/wiki/Mithras" class="mw-redirect" title="Mithras">Mithraic</a> shrines (known as <a href="/wiki/Mithraeum" title="Mithraeum">Mithraea</a>) which existed along Hadrian\'s Wall were attacked and destroyed, presumably by Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> After the departure of the Romans it is generally presumed that Christianity would have survived among the Bythonic enclaves such as Strathclyde, but retreated as the pagan Anglo-Saxons advanced, with their gods <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr" title="T\xc3\xbdr">Tiw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woden" class="mw-redirect" title="Woden">Woden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C4%ABg" class="mw-redirect" title="Fr\xc4\xabg">Frig</a>, all of whom gave their names to days of the week, and <a href="/wiki/Eostre" class="mw-redirect" title="Eostre">Eostre</a>, whose name was appropriated for the spring festival of Easter. While British Christians continued to practice <a href="/wiki/Inhumation" class="mw-redirect" title="Inhumation">inhumation</a> without grave goods, the pagan <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> are visible in the archaeological record from their practice of cremation and burial in urns, accompanied by extensive grave goods, perhaps designed to accompany the dead to the afterlife.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> However, despite growing evidence of Anglian settlement in southern Scotland, only one such grave has been found, at <a href="/wiki/Dalmeny" title="Dalmeny">Dalmeny</a> in East Lothian.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The growth of Christianity in Scotland has been traditionally seen as dependent on Irish-Scots "Celtic" missionaries and to a lesser extent those from Rome and England. <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a> had its origins in the conversion of Ireland from late Roman Britain associated with <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Patrick">St. Patrick</a> in the 5th century. In the 6th century, monks from Ireland were operating on the British mainland. St Ninian is the figure associated with a monastery founded at <a href="/wiki/Whithorn" title="Whithorn">Whithorn</a> in what is now Galloway, although it is generally accepted that Ninian may be a later construct.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> St Columba left Ireland and founded the monastery at Iona off the West Coast of Scotland in 563 and from there carried out missions to the Scots of D\xc3\xa1l Riata and the Picts. It seems likely that both the Scots and Picts had already begun to convert to Christianity before this period.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Saint Patrick referred in a letter to "apostate Picts", suggesting that they had previously been Christian, while the poem <i><a href="/wiki/Y_Gododdin" title="Y Gododdin">Y Gododdin</a></i>, set in the early 6th century does not remark on the Picts as pagans.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Conversion of the Pictish \xc3\xa9lite seems likely to have run over a considerable period, beginning in the 5th century and not complete until the 7th.<sup id="cite_ref-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among the key indicators of Christianisation are long-<a href="/wiki/Cist" title="Cist">cist</a> cemeteries that generally indicate Christian burials due to their East-West orientation,<sup id="cite_ref-Proudfoot_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proudfoot-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> although this correlation has been challenged by recent research.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> These burials are found between the end of the Roman era and the 7th century, after which point they become rarer. They are concentrated strongly in eastern Scotland south of the Tay, in <a href="/wiki/Angus,_Scotland" title="Angus, Scotland">Angus</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Mearns" class="mw-redirect" title="The Mearns">the Mearns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lothian" title="Lothian">Lothian</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Borders" class="mw-redirect" title="Borders">Borders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> It is generally accepted among scholars that place-name element <i>eccles-</i>, from the Brythonic word for church, represents evidence of the British church of the Roman and immediate post-Roman period, most of which are located in the south-west, south and east.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> About a dozen inscribed stones of the 5th and 6th centuries, beginning with the so-called Latinus stone of <a href="/wiki/Whithorn" title="Whithorn">Whithorn</a>, dating to c. 450, indicate Christianity through their dedications and are spread across southern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celtic_Christianity">Celtic Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Celtic Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fra_Angelico_052.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Fra_Angelico_052.jpg/170px-Fra_Angelico_052.jpg" width="170" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Fra_Angelico_052.jpg/255px-Fra_Angelico_052.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Fra_Angelico_052.jpg/340px-Fra_Angelico_052.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2536" data-file-height="2898" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fra_Angelico_052.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "Roman" <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsure</a>, in the shape of a crown, differing from the Irish tradition, where the hair above the forehead was shaved</div></div></div>\n<p>Celtic Christianity differed in some in respects from that based on Rome, most importantly on the issues of how <a href="/wiki/Easter_controversy" title="Easter controversy">Easter was calculated</a> and the method of <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsure</a>, but there were also differences in the rites of <a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">ordination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>. Celtic Christianity was heavily based on monasticism. Monasteries differed significantly from those on the continent, and were often an isolated collection of wooden huts surrounded by a wall. Because much of the Celtic world lacked the urban centres of the Roman world, bishoprics were often attached to abbeys. In the 5th, 6th and 7th centuries, Irish monks established monastic institutions in parts of modern-day Scotland. Monks from Iona, under <a href="/wiki/Aidan_of_Lindisfarne" title="Aidan of Lindisfarne">St. Aidan</a>, then founded the See of Lindisfarne in Anglian Northumbria.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1985pp77-89_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1985pp77-89-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The part of southern Scotland dominated by the Anglians in this period had a Bishopric established at <a href="/wiki/Abercorn" title="Abercorn">Abercorn</a> in West Lothian, and it is presumed that it would have adopted the leadership of Rome after the Synod of Whitby in 663, until the Battle of Dunnichen in 685, when the Bishop and his followers were ejected.<sup id="cite_ref-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> By this time the Roman system of calculating Easter and other reforms had already been adopted in much of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1985pp77-89_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1985pp77-89-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The Picts accepted the reforms of Rome under <a href="/wiki/Nechtan_mac_Der-Ilei" title="Nechtan mac Der-Ilei">Nechtan mac Der-Ilei</a> around 710.<sup id="cite_ref-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RitchieandRichie1991pp171-2-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The followers of Celtic traditions retreated to Iona and then to <a href="/wiki/Inishbofin,_County_Galway" title="Inishbofin, County Galway">Innishbofin</a> and the Western isles remained an outpost of Celtic practice for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1985pp77-89_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1985pp77-89-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Celtic Christianity continued to influence religion in England and across Europe into the late Middle Ages as part of the <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Hiberno-Scottish mission</a>, spreading Christianity, monasteries, art and theological ideas across the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viking_paganism">Viking paganism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Viking paganism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norse_pagan_worship" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse pagan worship">Norse pagan worship</a></div>\n<p>The Viking occupation of the islands and coastal regions of modern Scotland brought a return to pagan worship in those areas. Norse paganism had some of the same gods as had been worshipped by the Anglo-Saxons before their conversion and is thought to have been focused around a series of cults, involving gods, ancestors and spirits, with calendric and life cycle rituals often involving forms of sacrifice.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The paganism of the ruling Norse elite can be seen in goods found in 10th century graves in Shetland, Orkney and Caithness.<sup id="cite_ref-Lang2006p307_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lang2006p307-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> There is no contemporary account of the conversion of the Vikings in Scotland to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007p310-11_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007p310-11-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Historians have traditionally pointed to a process of conversion to Christianity among Viking colonies in Britain dated to the late 10th century, for which later accounts indicate that Viking earls accepted Christianity. However, there is evidence that conversion had begun before this point. There are a large number of isles called Pabbay or Papa in the Western and Northern Isles, which may indicate a "hermit\'s" or "priest\'s isle" from this period. Changes in patterns of grave goods and Viking place names using -kirk also suggest that the Christianity had begun to spread before the official conversion.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004pp67-8_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004pp67-8-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Later documentary evidence suggests that there was a Bishop operating in Orkney in the mid-9th century and more recently uncovered archaeological evidence, including explicitly Christian forms such as stone crosses,<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf2007p310-11_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf2007p310-11-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> suggest that Christian practice may have survived the Viking take over in parts of Orkney and Shetland and that the process of conversion may have begun before Christianity was officially accepted by Viking leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The continuity of Scottish Christianity may also explain the relatively rapid way in which Norse settlers were later assimilated into the religion.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art" title="Celtic art">Celtic art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Art in Medieval Scotland">Art in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aberlemno.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aberlemno.jpg/170px-Aberlemno.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aberlemno.jpg/255px-Aberlemno.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Aberlemno.jpg 2x" data-file-width="334" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aberlemno.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Class II Kirkyard stone c. 800, <a href="/wiki/Aberlemno" title="Aberlemno">Aberlemno</a></div></div></div>\n<p>From the 5th to the mid-9th centuries the art of the Picts is primarily known through stone sculpture, and a smaller number of pieces of metalwork, often of very high quality. After the conversion of the Picts and the cultural assimilation of Pictish culture into that of the Scots and Angles, elements of Pictish art became incorporated into the style known as <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a>, which was common over Britain and Ireland and became highly influential in continental Europe and contributed to the development of <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque</a> styles.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pictish_stones">Pictish stones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pictish stones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pictish_stones" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictish stones">Pictish stones</a></div>\n<p>About 250 Pictish stones survive and have been assigned by scholars to three classes.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Class I stones are those thought to date to the period up to the 7th century and are the most numerous group. The stones are largely unshaped and include incised symbols of animals including fish and the <a href="/wiki/Pictish_beast" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictish beast">Pictish beast</a>, everyday objects such as mirrors, combs and tuning forks and abstract symbols defined by names including V-rod, double disc and Z-rod. They are found from the Firth of Forth to Shetland. The greatest concentrations are in Sutherland, around modern Inverness and Aberdeen. Good examples include the <a href="/wiki/Dunrobin_Castle" title="Dunrobin Castle">Dunrobin</a> (Sutherland) and <a href="/wiki/Aberlemno" title="Aberlemno">Aberlemno</a> stones (<a href="/wiki/Angus,_Scotland" title="Angus, Scotland">Angus</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Class II stones are carefully shaped slabs dating after the arrival of Christianity in the 8th and 9th centuries, with a cross on one face and a wide range of symbols on the reverse. In smaller numbers than Class I stones, they predominate in southern Pictland, in Perth, Angus and Fife. Good examples include <a href="/wiki/Glamis" title="Glamis">Glamis</a> 2, which contains a finely executed <a href="/wiki/Celtic_cross" title="Celtic cross">Celtic cross</a> on the main face with two opposing male figures, a centaur, cauldron, deer head and a triple disc symbol and Cossans, Angus, which shows a high-prowed Pictish boat with oarsmen and a figure facing forward in the prow.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Class III stones are thought to overlap chronologically with Class II stones. Most are elaborately shaped and incised cross-slabs, some with figurative scenes, but lacking idiomatic Pictish symbols. They are widely distributed but predominate in the southern Pictish areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-Campbell1998pp.7-8-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pictish_metalwork">Pictish metalwork</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pictish metalwork">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg/220px-ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg/330px-ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg/440px-ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1936" data-file-height="938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ArtCelteScotlandFibuleExpoClunydetail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Rogart brooch, National Museums of Scotland, FC2. Pictish <a href="/wiki/Penannular_brooch" class="mw-redirect" title="Penannular brooch">penannular brooch</a>, 8th century, silver with gilding and glass.  Classified as Fowler H3 type.</div></div></div>\n<p>Metalwork has been found throughout Pictland; the Picts appear to have had a considerable amount of silver available, probably from raiding further south, or the payment of subsidies to keep them from doing so. The very large hoard of late Roman <a href="/wiki/Hacksilver" title="Hacksilver">hacksilver</a> found at <a href="/wiki/Traprain_Law" title="Traprain Law">Traprain Law</a> may have originated in either way. The largest hoard of early Pictish metalwork was found in 1819 at Norrie\'s Law in Fife, but unfortunately much was dispersed and melted down.<sup id="cite_ref-Youngs1989pp26-8_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Youngs1989pp26-8-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Over ten heavy silver chains, some over 0.5 metres (2&#160;ft) long, have been found from this period; the double-linked <a href="/wiki/Whitecleuch_Chain" title="Whitecleuch Chain">Whitecleuch Chain</a> is one of only two that have a penannular ring, with symbol decoration including enamel, which shows how these were probably used as "choker" necklaces.<sup id="cite_ref-Youngs1989pp26-8_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Youngs1989pp26-8-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/St_Ninian%27s_Isle" title="St Ninian&#39;s Isle">St Ninian\'s Isle</a> Treasure contains perhaps the best collection of Pictish forms.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish-Scots_art">Irish-Scots art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Irish-Scots art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The kingdom of D\xc3\xa1l Riata has been seen as a cross-roads between the artistic styles of the Picts and those of Ireland, with which the Scots settlers in what is now Argyll kept close contacts. This can be seen in representations found in excavations of the fortress of Dunadd, which combine Pictish and Irish elements.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> This included extensive evidence for the production of high status jewellery and moulds from the 7th century that indicate the production of pieces similar to the <a href="/wiki/Hunterston_brooch" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunterston brooch">Hunterston brooch</a>, found in Ayrshire, but with elements that suggest Irish origins. These and other finds, including a trumpet spiral decorated hanging bowl disc and a stamped animal decoration (or pressblech), perhaps from a bucket or drinking horn, indicate the ways in which D\xc3\xa1l Riata was one of the locations where the Insular style was developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lane2001_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lane2001-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In the 8th and 9th centuries the Pictish elite adopted true penannular <a href="/wiki/Brooch" title="Brooch">brooches</a> with lobed terminals from Ireland. Some older Irish <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-penannular" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-penannular">pseudo-penannular</a> brooches were adapted to the Pictish style, for example the Breadalbane Brooch (British Museum). The 8th century <a href="/wiki/Monymusk_Reliquary" title="Monymusk Reliquary">Monymusk Reliquary</a> has elements of Pictish and Irish style.<sup id="cite_ref-Youngs1989pp109-1123_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Youngs1989pp109-1123-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insular_art">Insular art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Insular art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg/170px-KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg/255px-KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg/340px-KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="1012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Opening page from the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Insular art, or Hiberno-Saxon art, is the name given to the common style produced in Scotland, Britain and Anglo-Saxon England from the 7th century, with the combining of Celtic and Anglo-Saxon forms.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Surviving examples of Insular art are found in metalwork, carving, but mainly in <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a>. Surfaces are highly decorated with intricate patterning, with no attempt to give an impression of depth, volume or recession. The best examples include the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Durrow" title="Book of Durrow">Book of Durrow</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carpet_pages" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpet pages">Carpet pages</a> are a characteristic feature of Insular manuscripts, although <a href="/wiki/Historiated_initial" title="Historiated initial">historiated initials</a> (an Insular invention), <a href="/wiki/Canon_table" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon table">canon tables</a> and figurative miniatures, especially <a href="/wiki/Evangelist_portrait" title="Evangelist portrait">Evangelist portraits</a>, are also common. The finest era of the style was brought to an end by the disruption to monastic centres and aristocratic life of the Viking raids in the late 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The influence of Insular art affected all subsequent European Medieval art, especially in the decorative elements of Romanesque and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_in_Medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Architecture in Medieval Scotland">Architecture in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<p>For the period after the departure of the Romans there is evidence of a series of new forts, often smaller "nucleated" constructions compared with those from the Iron Age,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> sometimes utilising major geographical features, as at <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Castle" title="Edinburgh Castle">Edinburgh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Castle" title="Dumbarton Castle">Dunbarton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> All the northern British peoples utilised different forms of fort and the determining factors in construction were local terrain, building materials, and politico-military needs.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The first identifiable king of the Picts, Bridei mac Maelchon had his base at the fort of <a href="/wiki/Craig_Phadrig" title="Craig Phadrig">Craig Phadrig</a> near modern <a href="/wiki/Inverness" title="Inverness">Inverness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Haywoodp2004p116_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haywoodp2004p116-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> overkingdom of D\xc3\xa1l Riata was probably ruled from the fortress of <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> now near Kilmartin in <a href="/wiki/Argyll_and_Bute" title="Argyll and Bute">Argyll and Bute</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion1_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of Christianity into Scotland from Ireland from the sixth century, led to the construction of the first churches. These may originally have been wooden, like that excavated at <a href="/wiki/Whithorn" title="Whithorn">Whithorn</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> but most of those for which evidence survives from this era are basic masonry-built churches, beginning on the west coast and islands and spreading south and east.<sup id="cite_ref-Maxwell2005pp22-3_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2005pp22-3-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gododdin1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gododdin1.jpg/170px-Gododdin1.jpg" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gododdin1.jpg/255px-Gododdin1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gododdin1.jpg/340px-Gododdin1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="969" data-file-height="1305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gododdin1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the Book of Aneirin shows the first part of the text from the Gododdin c. sixth century</div></div></div>\n<p>Early chapels tended to have square-ended converging walls, similar to Irish chapels of this period.<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p8_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p8-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Medieval parish church architecture in Scotland was typically much less elaborate than in England, with many churches remaining simple oblongs, without <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transepts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aisle" title="Aisle">aisles</a>, and often without towers. In the Highlands they were often even simpler, many built of rubble masonry and sometimes indistinguishable from the outside from houses or farm buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Monasteries also differed significantly from those on the continent, and were often an isolated collection of wooden huts surrounded by a wall.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1985pp77-89_90-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1985pp77-89-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Eileach_an_Naoimh" title="Eileach an Naoimh">Eileach an Naoimh</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner Hebrides</a> there are huts, a chapel, <a href="/wiki/Refectory" title="Refectory">refectory</a>, guest house, barns and other buildings. Most of these were made of timber and <a href="/wiki/Wattle_(construction)" title="Wattle (construction)">wattle</a> construction and probably thatched with heather and turves. They were later rebuilt in stone, with underground cells and circular <a href="/wiki/Cloch%C3%A1n" title="Cloch\xc3\xa1n">"beehive" huts</a> like those used in Ireland. Similar sites have been excavated on Bute, Orkney and Shetland.<sup id="cite_ref-West1985p8_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West1985p8-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> From the eighth century more sophisticated buildings emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-Maxwell2005pp22-3_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2005pp22-3-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_literature_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Scottish literature in the Middle Ages">Scottish literature in the Middle Ages</a></div><p>\nMuch of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Welsh-language_literature" title="Welsh-language literature">Welsh literature</a> was actually composed in or near the country now called Scotland, although only written down in Wales much later. These include <i>The <a href="/wiki/Y_Gododdin" title="Y Gododdin">Gododdin</a></i>, considered the earliest surviving verse from Scotland, which is attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">bard</a> <a href="/wiki/Aneirin" title="Aneirin">Aneirin</a>, said to have been resident in Gododdin in the 6th century, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gwen_Ystrad" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Gwen Ystrad">Battle of Gwen Ystrad</a></i> attributed to <a href="/wiki/Taliesin" title="Taliesin">Taliesin</a>, traditionally thought to be a bard at the court of Rheged in roughly the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> There are also religious works in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Gaelic</a> including the <i>Elegy for St Columba</i> by Dallan Forgaill (c. 597) and "In Praise of St Columba" by Beccan mac Luigdech of Rum (c. 677).<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> they include a "Prayer for Protection" (attributed to St Mugint) (c. mid-6th century) and <i><a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Latin#Altus_Prosator" title="Hiberno-Latin">Altus Prosator</a></i> ("The High Creator", attributed to St Columba) (c. 597).<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In Old English there is <i>The <a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Rood" title="Dream of the Rood">Dream of the Rood</a></i>, from which lines are found on the <a href="/wiki/Ruthwell_Cross" title="Ruthwell Cross">Ruthwell Cross</a>, making it the only surviving fragment of <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_(Anglo-Saxon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbrian (Anglo-Saxon)">Northumbrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> from early Medieval Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Lang2006pp14-15-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Lang2006pp14-15_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">L. R. Laing, <i>The Archaeology of Celtic Britain and Ireland, c. AD 400\xe2\x80\x931200</i> (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2006), <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r861714446">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px dotted;cursor:help}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-54740-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-54740-7">0-521-54740-7</a>, pp. 14\xe2\x80\x9315.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">C. Haywood, <i>The Celts: Bronze Age to New Age</i> (London: Pearson Education, 2004), <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-582-50578-X" title="Special:BookSources/0-582-50578-X">0-582-50578-X</a>, p. 116.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">A. P.
